<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2014 46:6"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-05-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Yu-Xian Zhu, Jun Wang, Shuxun Yu and colleagues report sequencing and assembly of the genome of cultivated cotton, Gossypium arboreum. Comparison with the Gossypium raimondii genome sequence provides insights into genome evolution and speciation, and identifies two shared whole-genome duplication events occurring before the speciation event around 2&#8211;13 million years ago. The complex allotetraploid nature of the cotton genome (AADD; 2n = 52) makes genetic, genomic and functional analyses extremely challenging. Here we sequenced and assembled the Gossypium arboreum (AA; 2n = 26) genome, a putative contributor of the A subgenome. A total of 193.6 Gb of clean sequence covering the genome by 112.6-fold was obtained by paired-end sequencing. We further anchored and oriented 90.4% of the assembly on 13 pseudochromosomes and found that 68.5% of the genome is occupied by repetitive DNA sequences. We predicted 41,330 protein-coding genes in G. arboreum. Two whole-genome duplications were shared by G. arboreum and Gossypium raimondii before speciation. Insertions of long terminal repeats in the past 5 million years are responsible for the twofold difference in the sizes of these genomes. Comparative transcriptome studies showed the key role of the nucleotide binding site (NBS)-encoding gene family in resistance to Verticillium dahliae and the involvement of ethylene in the development of cotton fiber cells."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The complex allotetraploid nature of the cotton genome (AADD; 2<i>n</i> = 52) makes genetic, genomic and functional analyses extremely challenging. Here we sequenced and assembled the <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (AA; 2<i>n</i> = 26) genome, a putative contributor of the A subgenome. A total of 193.6 Gb of clean sequence covering the genome by 112.6-fold was obtained by paired-end sequencing. We further anchored and oriented 90.4% of the assembly on 13 pseudochromosomes and found that 68.5% of the genome is occupied by repetitive DNA sequences. We predicted 41,330 protein-coding genes in <i>G. arboreum</i>. Two whole-genome duplications were shared by <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> before speciation. Insertions of long terminal repeats in the past 5 million years are responsible for the twofold difference in the sizes of these genomes. Comparative transcriptome studies showed the key role of the nucleotide binding site (NBS)-encoding gene family in resistance to <i>Verticillium dahliae</i> and the involvement of ethylene in the development of cotton fiber cells.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Gossypium</i> includes 46 diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2x = 26) and 5 well-established and 1 purported tetraploid (2<i>n</i> = 4x = 52) species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wendel, J. &amp; Albert, V.A. Phylogenetics of the cotton genus (Gossypium): characterstate weighted parsimony analysis of chloroplast-DNA restriction site data and its systematic and biogeographic implications. Syst. Bot. 17, 115–143 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17936e902">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Seijo, G. Gossypium ekmanianum (Malvaceae), algodon silvestre de la Republica Dominicana. Bonplandia 17, 55–63 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17936e905">2</a></sup>. It has been proposed that all diploid cotton species may have evolved from a common ancestor that subsequently diversified to produce eight groups, including groups A–G and K<sup>3</sup>. Allopolyploid cotton may have appeared in the last 1–2 million years through hybridization and subsequent polyploidization events between the A- and D-subgenome progenitors. All tetraploid cotton species came from interspecific hybridization between the A-genome species <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and the D-genome species <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I. &amp; Cronn, R. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton, Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A.H.) 3–22 (Springer-Verlag, New York, 2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17936e922">3</a></sup>. The A-genome species are cultivated, whereas the D-genome species do not produce spinnable fiber. Although <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>) are the putative donor species for the A and D chromosome groups, respectively, tetraploid cotton species differ greatly with respect to plant morphology as well as economic characteristics, including fiber production, oil content and disease resistance. Furthermore, <i>G. arboreum</i> (1,746 Mb/1C) has a genome size that is almost twice that of <i>G. raimondii</i> (885 Mb/1C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17936e943">4</a></sup>.</p><p>In the current study, assisted by a high-resolution genetic map, we anchored and oriented 90.4% of the <i>G. arboreum</i> assembled scaffolds on 13 pseudochromosomes. This assembly was compared with that of <i>G. raimondii</i> to understand possible paths for genome evolution and species divergence. A highly homozygous single-seed descendant, derived from 18 successive generations of self-fertilization, of the cultivated diploid cotton <i>Shixiya1</i> (<i>SXY1</i>) was used for DNA sequencing. Assembly of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome and comparative studies with the genome of <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e968">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17936e971">6</a></sup> may provide new insights into the process of divergence among polyploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17936e975">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17936e978">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17936e981">9</a></sup>. We believe that the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome offers a diploid reference for the analysis of cotton agronomic traits, such as fiber quality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shi, Y.-H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17936e988">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Qin, Y.-M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692–3704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17936e991">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Pang, C.-Y. et al. Comparative proteomics indicates that biosynthesis of pectic precursors is important for cotton fiber and Arabidopsis root hair elongation. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 9, 2019–2033 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17936e994">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Qin, Y.-M. &amp; Zhu, Y.-X. How cotton fibers elongate: a tale of linear cell-growth mode. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 14, 106–111 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17936e997">13</a></sup> and disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pegg, G.F. &amp; Brady, B.L. in Verticillium Wilts 552 (CABI, New York, 2002)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1001">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Khadi, B.M., Santhy, V. &amp; Yadav, M.S. in Cotton: Biotechnological Advances (ed. Zehr, U.B.) 15–44 (Springer, New York, 2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1004">15</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We sequenced and assembled the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome using the whole-genome shotgun approach. In brief, a total of 371.5 Gb of raw paired-end Illumina reads was generated by sequencing genome shotgun libraries with different fragment lengths that ranged from 180 bp to 40 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 1</a>). After filtering out low-quality reads, 193.6 Gb of high-quality sequence was obtained and used for the <i>de novo</i> assembly process (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 2</a>). We next used 33,454 BAC end sequences (16,727 pairs; approximately 1-fold physical coverage) to improve the assembly. Over 90% of our BAC clones contained inserts of ≥50,000 bp in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig6">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). In-depth analysis showed that all scaffolds were supported by multiple (&gt;80) paired-end linkages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig7">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). The number of scaffolds decreased in libraries with increasing insert size (&gt;2,000 bp). Using <i>K</i>-mer distribution analysis, the genome size was estimated to be 1,724 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig8">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), which is consistent with a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1045">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Our final assembly, performed with SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1052">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1055">17</a></sup>, showed that the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome is 1,694 Mb in total length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Ninety percent of the assembly fell into 2,724 superscaffolds that were &gt;148 kb in length, with the largest scaffold being 5.9 Mb. N50 (the size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) for the contigs and scaffolds was 72 kb and 666 kb, respectively. Data quality was assessed by comparison with Sanger sequencing–derived <i>G. arboreum</i> sequences and by mapping available ESTs to the assembly. Nineteen of the twenty completely sequenced <i>G. arboreum</i> BAC clones available from GenBank were recovered in our assembly, with &gt;98% sequence identity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Of the 55,894 Sanger sequencing–derived <i>G. arboreum</i> ESTs (&gt;200 bp in length) available from NCBI, 96.37% were detected in our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Using a restriction site–associated DNA (RAD) linkage map that we constructed during the current study based on 24,569 codominant SNP markers, 1,532 Mb or 90.4% of the assembled genome was anchored and oriented on 13 pseudochromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig9">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 5</a>), which contained 97.1% of the predicted gene models. Collinear relationships existed between the SNP markers and most of the assembled <i>G. arboreum</i> pseudochromosomes, which indicates that our assembly is of high quality (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig10">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Collinearity was also observed between our <i>G. arboreum</i> assembly and the tetraploid genetic map reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Rong, J. et al. A 3347-locus genetic recombination map of sequence-tagged sites reveals features of genome organization, transmission and evolution of cotton (Gossypium). Genetics 166, 389–417 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1100">18</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig11">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 6</a>). The GC content of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome was comparable to those for the genomes of <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1116">5</a></sup>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1122">19</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics of <i>G. arboreum</i> genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2987/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Characterization of the G. arboreum cotton genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Characterization of the <i>G. arboreum</i> cotton genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="858"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Percent coverage of TEs in non-overlapping windows (window size = 500 kb). Outer tick marks show the calculated lengths of 13 <i>G. arboreum</i> pseudochromosomes. (<b>b</b>) Gene density estimated on the basis of the number of genes in non-overlapping 500-kb windows. (<b>c</b>) Transcription state. The transcript level for each gene was estimated by averaging values of reads per kilobase of mapped cDNA per million reads (RPKM) from different tissues in non-overlapping 500-kb windows. (<b>d</b>) Marker density represented by the number of SNPs in non-overlapping 500-kb windows. (<b>e</b>) GC content estimated on the basis of the percentage of G+C nucleotides in 500-kb non-overlapping windows.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM58">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>We performed genome annotation by combining results obtained from <i>ab initio</i> prediction, homology search and transcriptome alignment. As much as 68.5% of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome was composed of various types of repeat sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 7</a>). We believe that this genome has the greatest amount of repeat-containing sequences among sequenced eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1554">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1557">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1560">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1563">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1566">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1569">24</a></sup>. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons accounted for 95.12% of all repeat sequences. In comparison with the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1576">5</a></sup>, the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome had noticeable proliferation of <i>Gorge</i> elements, whereas LTR <i>Copia</i> elements tended to accumulate in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 8</a>), as has been suggested previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hawkins, J.S., Kim, H., Nason, J.D., Wing, R.A. &amp; Wendel, J.F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252–1261 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1596">25</a></sup>. LTR retrotransposons in <i>G. arboreum</i> appeared to have inserted randomly along each chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>), in a pattern substantially different from the ones observed for their insertion sites in soybean and potato, in which LTRs are clustered near the centromere and are found less frequently near telomeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1606">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1609">21</a></sup>.</p><p>A total of 41,330 protein-coding genes were identified in the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome, with an average transcript size of 2,533 bp (as determined by GLEAN) and a mean of 4.6 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 9</a>). The genome encoded 431 microRNAs (miRNAs), 10,464 rRNAs, 2,289 tRNAs and 7,619 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Among the annotated genes, 85.64% encoded proteins that showed homology to proteins in the TrEMBL database, and 68.71% were identified in InterPro (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Over 96% of predicted coding sequences were supported by transcriptome sequencing data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 9</a>), which indicated high accuracy of <i>G. arboreum</i> gene predictions from the genome sequence. Orthologous clustering of the <i>G. arboreum</i> proteome with 3 closely related plant genomes identified 11,699 gene families in common, with 739 gene families that were present specifically in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig12">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome evolution</h3><p>The current descendants of the two diploid cotton species that hybridized to create all cultivated allopolyploid cotton species, <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>), exhibit a twofold difference in genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1672">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhang, H.-B., Li, Y., Wang, B. &amp; Chee, P.W. Recent advances in cotton genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2008, 742304 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1675">26</a></sup>. Molecular phylogenetic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wikström, N., Savolainen, V. &amp; Chase, M.W. Evolution of the angiosperms: calibrating the family tree. Proc. Biol. Sci. 268, 2211–2220 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1680">27</a></sup> suggested a divergence time for <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> of 2–13 million years ago, with their common ancestor having diverged from <i>T. cacao</i> 18–58 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic and evolutionary analysis of the Gossypium genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Phylogenetic and evolutionary analysis of the <i>Gossypium</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="471"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Phylogenetic analysis indicated that <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> separated <span class="stix">∼</span>5 million years ago (mya). (<b>b</b>) Fourfold-degenerate analyses suggested that the <i>Gossypium</i> genomes might have undergone two WGD events and one speciation event. (<b>c</b>) Large numbers of syntenic blocks were found when comparing either the <i>G. raimondii</i> (<i>Gr</i>) or <i>G. arboreum</i> (<i>Ga</i>) genome with the genome of <i>T. cacao</i> (<i>Tc</i>). Numbered rectangles represent the chromosomes.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM59">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We scanned the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome for syntenic gene blocks identified on the basis of protein-coding sequence similarity. An age distribution was calculated for all duplicate gene pairs on the basis of the number of transversions at fourfold-degenerate sites. Using 1,917 paralogous gene pairs of similar age and excluding tandem or local duplications, a large peak centered around 0.17 synonymous transversions per site and a second peak at about 0.54 synonymous transversions per site were observed in both the <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> genomes, indicating that a recent and an ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event occurred in cotton (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Both of these WGD events in <i>G. arboreum</i> coincided with those in <i>G. raimondii</i>, which were estimated to have occurred at 13–20 and 115–146 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1786">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J.S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1789">28</a></sup>. The ancient duplication event corresponds to the ancient hexaploidization event that is shared among the eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1793">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J.A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1796">30</a></sup>. Using a whole-genome alignment approach, we found that both the <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> genomes showed close collinear relationships with the genome of <i>T. cacao</i>. There were 209 and 295 collinear blocks covering 82% and 66% of the <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 11</a>). When the <i>G. raimondii</i> assembly reported by Paterson <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1829">6</a></sup> was used for the analysis, a slightly lower number of collinear blocks with higher genome coverage were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Approximately 50% of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome was aligned with two segments in each of the cotton genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>), undoubtedly suggesting the existence of a <i>Gossypium</i>-specific WGD event after speciation from the <i>T. cacao</i> lineage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Orthology maps and retrotransposition analysis</h3><p>Whole-genome alignment also identified 68,863 orthologous hits (anchors) on the 2 cotton genomes, including 33,229 genes for <i>G. arboreum</i> and 34,204 genes for <i>G. raimondii</i>. These anchors spanned 17.1% of the A genome and 35.0% of the D genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 12</a>). When the <i>G. raimondii</i> assembly by Paterson <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1872">6</a></sup> was used for the analysis, we observed a total of 63,810 anchors that spanned 23% of the A genome and 48% of the D genome, indicating an improvement in data quality for the later assembly in this respect (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Using these anchors, we found that the 2 cotton genomes shared 780 syntenic blocks that covered 73% and 88% of the assembled chromosomes in <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Chromosomes 1, 4–6 and 9–13 were highly collinear in the two cotton species, whereas large-scale rearrangements were observed on chromosomes 2 and 3 of <i>G. raimondii</i> and deletions and insertions were observed on chromosomes 7 and 8 of <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Whole-genome alignment and expansion of the G. arboreum genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Whole-genome alignment and expansion of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>,<b>b</b>) Whole-genome alignments of <i>G. arboreum</i> on <i>G. raimondii</i> (<b>a</b>) and of <i>G. raimondii</i> on <i>G. arboreum</i> (<b>b</b>). Chromosomes from both genomes were plotted according to their assembled lengths. Each of the 13 chromosomes in <b>a</b> and <b>b</b> is designated by color; white blocks represent regions that could not be aligned. Only syntenic blocks of ≥2 Mb in length are shown. (<b>c</b>) Analysis of LTR number and insertion time in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<i>Ga</i>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (<i>Gr</i>). Bars (left <i>y</i> axis) indicate the number of LTRs inserted at any specific time point, whereas curves (right <i>y</i> axis) represent the accumulated total number of LTRs throughout evolution. (<b>d</b>) In-depth study of a microsyntenic block on chromosomes 7 of <i>G. arboreum</i> (Ga07) and <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1976">5</a></sup> (Gr07). Yellow bars, gene models; green lines, syntenic gene pairs; blue bars, TEs present at the corresponding chromosomal locations in each genome. The total number of genes and TEs found in each region is shown beneath each schematic.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM60">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Expansions of transposable element (TE) families have been reported in the genome of <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e1999">5</a></sup>. In <i>G. arboreum</i>, two major clusters of retrotransposition activity were found at 0–0.5 and 3.5–4.5 million years ago, with two additional minor sets of retrotransposition activity present around 1.0 and 7.0–8.0 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). As a result, the difference in LTR numbers between these two cotton species showed two separate peaks at <span class="stix">∼</span>0–0.5 and <span class="stix">∼</span>3.5 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Data analysis indicated that the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome tended to harbor more LTRs inserted during the last 0.5 million years, whereas extremely low LTR activity was observed in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome over the same period of time. Thus, LTR activities substantially contributed to the twofold increase in the size of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome, as previously suggested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Swigonová, Z. et al. Close split of sorghum and maize genome progenitors. Genome Res. 14, 1916–1923 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2022">31</a></sup>. We confirmed our assertion by analyzing microsyntenic blocks on chromosome 7 of both <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> that were 3.5 and 1.5 Mb in length, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig13">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). The gene loci in these syntenic blocks were highly collinear, with 191 loci in <i>G. raimondii</i> and 216 loci in <i>G. arboreum</i>. However, 4,098 TEs were identified in <i>G. arboreum</i>, whereas only 1,542 TEs were found in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). Close to 58% of the TEs reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig3">Figure 3d</a> were <i>Gorge</i> elements in <i>G. arboreum</i>, whereas these elements represented only <span class="stix">∼</span>21% of TEs in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Analysis of potential <i>Verticillium</i> wilt resistance genes</h3><p><i>Verticillium</i> wilt is a widespread and destructive cotton disease caused by the soil-borne fungus pathogen <i>V. dahliae</i>. <i>G. raimondii</i> is nearly immune to the pathogen, whereas <i>G. arboreum</i> is easily susceptible<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pegg, G.F. &amp; Brady, B.L. in Verticillium Wilts 552 (CABI, New York, 2002)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2094">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Khadi, B.M., Santhy, V. &amp; Yadav, M.S. in Cotton: Biotechnological Advances (ed. Zehr, U.B.) 15–44 (Springer, New York, 2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2097">15</a></sup>. Genes related to disease resistance (R genes) have critical roles during various stages of disease development in plants. Thus far, most of the cloned R genes encode NBS domains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2101">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Collier, S.M. &amp; Moffett, P. NB-LRRs work a “bait and switch” on pathogens. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 521–529 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2104">32</a></sup>. We compared the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome to the <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2114">5</a></sup> and <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2120">19</a></sup> genome sequences to identify differences in the expression patterns of NBS domain–encoding genes. We identified 391, 280 and 302 such genes in the genomes of <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). <i>T. cacao</i> is also susceptible to <i>Verticillium</i> wilt. Phylogenetic analysis predicted that there were 192 ancestral NBS-encoding genes in the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of cotton and cocoa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig14">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). The greatest differences in the numbers of NBS-encoding gene family members in these two cotton species existed in the TN and TNL subfamilies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4b,c</a>), whereas the number of genes in the N subfamily remained relatively unchanged (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>). Tandem duplications seemed to have a significant role in the expansion of the NBS-encoding gene family in <i>G. raimondii</i> after its divergence from <i>G. arboreum</i> <span class="stix">∼</span>5 million years ago, and segmental loss contributed to its contraction in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>). Quantitative RT-PCR (qRT-PCR) analysis of members of the TNL and TN subfamilies showed substantial gene expansion in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome and indicated that many orthologs that were not present in <i>G. arboreum</i> responded to the pathogen soon after infection. All early pathogen-responsive genes came from the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Fig. 4f</a>), suggesting that expansion and contraction in the numbers of NBS-encoding genes in different cotton species may have altered their resistance to <i>V. dahliae</i>. Less than 20% of the NBS-encoding genes from each of the diploid cotton species could be aligned with available <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> ESTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 15a</a>). Similarly, only 9 of the 53 TN and TNL subfamily members reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig4">Figure 4</a> could be amplified in the tetraploid genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 15b</a>). These results indicate that NBS-encoding genes have evolved rapidly in either diploid or allotetraploid cotton species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of NBS-encoding genes and potential molecular targets for resistance to Verticillium wilt."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Analysis of NBS-encoding genes and potential molecular targets for resistance to <i>Verticillium</i> wilt.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="371"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Analysis of gene numbers in all NBS-encoding disease resistance gene families in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<i>Ga</i>), <i>G. raimondii</i> (<i>Gr</i>) and <i>T. cacao</i> (<i>Tc</i>). (<b>b</b>–<b>d</b>) Analysis of gene numbers in the TN subfamily (<b>b</b>), the TNL subfamily (<b>c</b>) and the N subfamily (<b>d</b>). Numbers in circles represent the number of family members in each genome, and gene numbers for the MRCA are shown in rectangles. Numbers with plus and minus signs indicate the numbers of duplicated and deleted genes, respectively. (<b>e</b>) Example of tandem repeat and gene deletion of several TN subfamily members in <i>G. arboreum</i>, <i>G. raimondii and T. cacao</i>. Solid lines link orthologous genes on different chromosomes, and dashed lines represent inferred orthologous relationships plus putative deletions. Sections of <i>G. arboreum</i> chromosome 3 (Ga03) and <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosome 7 (Gr07) are enlarged for clarity, whereas all other pseudochromosomes are shown according to the scale bar. (<b>f</b>) qRT-PCR analysis of induced gene expression in the TN and TNL subfamilies upon <i>V. dahliae</i> infection. hpi, hours post-infection.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM61">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Ethylene is a key modulator of cotton fiber cell growth</h3><p>Ethylene is an important signaling molecule that promotes cotton fiber elongation in <i>G. hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shi, Y.-H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2297">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Qin, Y.-M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692–3704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2300">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Qin, Y.-M. &amp; Zhu, Y.-X. How cotton fibers elongate: a tale of linear cell-growth mode. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 14, 106–111 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2303">13</a></sup>. Products of the <i>1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase</i> (<i>ACO</i>) gene are involved in the last and rate-limiting step in ethylene biosynthesis during cotton fiber development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shi, Y.-H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2313">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Qin, Y.-M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692–3704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2316">11</a></sup>. In <i>G. raimondii</i> ovules collected at 3 days post-anthesis (dpa), the levels of <i>ACO1</i> transcripts were <span class="stix">∼</span>1,000-fold higher and the levels of <i>ACO3</i> transcripts were <span class="stix">∼</span>500-fold higher than in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). These two genes were chosen because they accounted for &gt;90% of all <i>ACO</i> transcripts during early fiber development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 16</a>). <i>GrACO1</i> and <i>GrACO3</i> showed 98% to 99% sequence identity in coding regions, respectively, with their counterparts in the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome (data not shown). Dot plots of promoter regions showed that a deletion of <span class="stix">∼</span>130 bp beginning at −470 bp relative to the transcription start site of <i>GaACO1</i> resulted in loss of a putative MYB-binding site (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). The pivotal role of MYB transcription factors in regulating cotton fiber development and secondary cell wall biosynthesis has previously been proposed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhong, R., Lee, C., Zhou, J., McCarthy, R.L. &amp; Ye, Z.-H. A battery of transcription factors involved in the regulation of secondary cell wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 20, 2763–2782 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2358">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Machado, A., Wu, Y., Yang, Y., Llewellyn, D.J. &amp; Dennis, E.S. The MYB transcription factor GhMYB25 regulates early fibre and trichome development. Plant J. 59, 52–62 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2361">34</a></sup>. Although <i>GaACO3</i> and <i>GrACO3</i> share one common MYB-binding site <span class="stix">∼</span>150 bp upstream of the transcriptional start site, two small segments of mismatch located around −750 bp to −800 bp led to loss of two additional MYB-binding sites in <i>G. arboreum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>). Very high levels of <i>ACO</i> transcripts in <i>G. raimondii</i> ovules in conjunction with an ethylene burst<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2385">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shi, Y.-H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2388">10</a></sup> might force an early fiber senescence phenotype, whereas the inactivation of <i>ACO</i> gene transcription in <i>G. arboreum</i> ovules might be responsible for the short-fiber phenotype in this species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>). When all <i>ACO</i> transcripts were quantified from different cotton species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 17</a> for primer sequences), we found that fiber-specific upregulation of <i>ACO</i> expression, as in the case of <i>G. hirsutum</i>, seems to be required for normal fiber growth. Inactivation or overproduction of <i>ACO</i>, as in <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>, respectively, might suppress fiber development. Further functional characterization of biosynthesis and signaling pathways is required to pin down such a fundamental role for ethylene in the regulation of the growth of cotton fiber cells.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Ethylene modulates the elongation of cotton fiber cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Ethylene modulates the elongation of cotton fiber cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="287"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparisons of <i>ACO1</i> and <i>ACO3</i> transcript levels during fiber cell development in <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>. For calculation of fold change in expression, we arbitrarily set the expression level of each <i>G. arboreum ACO</i> transcript collected at 0 dpa to 1. Error bars, s.d. for three independent experiments. (<b>b</b>,<b>c</b>) Dot plots showing consensus in the promoter regions of <i>ACO1</i> (<b>b</b>) and <i>ACO3</i> (<b>c</b>) in the two cotton species. M, putative MYB-binding site; T, putative TATA box; G, putative GATA box. (<b>d</b>) Schematics showing the role of ethylene production in the regulation of cotton fiber elongation. Promoters for <i>GrACO1</i> and <i>GrACO3</i> (top) are potentially bound to more MYB protein than their counterparts in <i>G. arboreum</i> (bottom). Curved lines represent corresponding <i>ACO</i> transcripts, and the gradient bar shows the amount of ethylene production from low (white) to high (black). Scale bars, 1.0 cm. (<b>e</b>) Comparison of total <i>ACO</i> transcript levels in three cotton species on the basis of qRT-PCR analysis (<i>n</i> = 3 biological replicates). As shown in <b>d</b>, <i>G. raimondii</i> produces almost no mature fiber and <i>G. arboreum</i> produces short fiber of <span class="stix">∼</span>1.5 cm in length; <i>G. hirsutum</i> produces fiber usually of &gt;3.0 cm in length.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM62">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Comparative genomic analysis showed that LTR insertions and expansions of LTR families contributed substantially to forming the double-sized <i>G. arboreum</i> genome relative to <i>G. raimondii</i>. The amount of sequence encompassing LTR-type retrotransposons increased from 348 Mb in <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2546">5</a></sup> to 1,145 Mb (or 67.6%) in <i>G. arboreum</i>, whereas the protein-coding capacities of these two species remained largely unchanged (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Typically, data obtained from the whole-genome shotgun approach in plants with genome sizes exceeding a few hundred megabases are quite difficult to assemble satisfactorily because of their high TE content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2560">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ling, H.-Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87–90 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2563">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kalendar, R., Tanskanen, J., Immonen, S., Nevo, E. &amp; Schulman, A.H. Genome evolution of wild barley (Hordeum spontaneum) by BARE-1 retrotransposon dynamics in response to sharp microclimatic divergence. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 6603–6607 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2566">37</a></sup>. Here 90.4% of the 1,694-Mb <i>G. arboreum</i> genome, which is the largest of all sequenced eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2573">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2576">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2579">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2582">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2585">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2588">24</a></sup>, was anchored and oriented. Problems related to high TE content were circumvented by using a high-density genetic map that involved 154 F<sub>2</sub> RAD lines. A previous study showed an association between LTR number and ecogeography in barley, with more LTR copies present in the genomes of plants from sites with greater environmental stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kalendar, R., Tanskanen, J., Immonen, S., Nevo, E. &amp; Schulman, A.H. Genome evolution of wild barley (Hordeum spontaneum) by BARE-1 retrotransposon dynamics in response to sharp microclimatic divergence. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 6603–6607 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2594">37</a></sup>. All wild <i>Gossypium</i> species seem to be adapted to arid and stressful environments. The different number of LTRs in <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> does not support the ecogeography hypothesis. There must be other unknown mechanisms and causes for the dramatic increase in the number of LTRs in the domesticated species <i>G. arboreum</i>.</p><p>Qualitative differences in several NBS-encoding subfamilies and in <i>ACO</i> gene expression between the <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> genomes were observed. Our data thus identify prime targets in deciphering the molecular mechanisms that control resistance to <i>Verticillium</i> wilt and fiber cell development. We suggest that this <i>G. arboreum</i> genome will be an essential reference for the assembly of tetraploid cotton genomes and for evolutionary studies of <i>Gossypium</i> species. It also provides an essential tool for the identification, isolation and manipulation of important cotton genes conferring agronomic traits for molecular breeding and genetic improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Strain selection.</h3><p>We used DNA samples prepared from a specific genetic line (cultivar <i>Shixiya1</i>, <i>SXY1</i>) of the cultivated diploid cotton <i>G. arboreum</i> for sequencing and assembly. This line was brought to near homozygosity after 18 successive generations of self-fertilization. <i>G. arboreum</i> cv. <i>Shixiya1</i> (<i>SXY1</i>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> D5-3 (CMD#10) plants were maintained in the National Wild Cotton Nursery in Sanya, Hainan Province, China.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DNA extraction, library construction and sequencing.</h3><p>Fresh young leaves were collected, immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −80 °C until DNA extraction. We used the standard phenol-chloroform method for DNA extraction with RNase A and proteinase K treatment to prevent RNA and protein contamination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shi, Y.-H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651–664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2675">10</a></sup>. Genomic libraries were prepared following the manufacturer's standard instructions and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. To construct paired-end libraries, DNA was fragmented by nebulization with compressed nitrogen gas, and DNA ends were blunted before adding an A base to each 3′ end. DNA adaptors with a single T-base 3′-end overhang were ligated to the above products. Ligation products were purified on 0.5%, 1% or 2% agarose gels, which each targeted a specific range of insert sizes. We constructed <i>G. arboreum</i> genome sequencing libraries with insert sizes of 180 bp, 250 bp, 350 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb and 40 kb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly.</h3><p>All sequences were assembled with SOAPdenovo software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2690">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2693">17</a></sup>. Unusable reads were filtered out before assembly, including (i) reads that contained sequences with &gt;10% “N” bases, (ii) reads with low-quality data (i.e., ≤7) for 65% of bases for short insert sizes or 80% of bases for long insert sizes, (iii) reads that contained &gt;10 bp of adaptor sequence, (iv) reads with &gt;10 bp that overlapped between two ends of reads of short insert size and (v) reads with identical sequences at the two ends.</p><p>The 193.6 Gb of clean data were assembled to contigs and scaffolds using the de Bruijn graph–based assembler of SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2700">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2703">17</a></sup> with the following four steps.</p><p><i>Constructing K-mer graphs</i>. SOAPdenovo split reads with an insert size of &lt;1,000 bases into <i>K</i>-mers to use in constructing the de Bruijn graphs. The parameters of this step in the cotton genome assembly were as follows: SOAPdenovo-127mer pregraph –s cotton_0308.lib –K 75 –R –p 24 –D –o GOSakoD&gt;pregraph.log.</p><p><i>Building contig</i>s. SOAPdenovo built contigs using the simplified de Bruijn graphs. The parameters of this step in the cotton genome assembly were as follows: SOAPdenovo-127mer contig –g GOSakoD –R &gt;contig.log.</p><p><i>Linking scaffold</i>s. SOAPdenovo aligned paired-end reads on the contig sequences. We subsequently calculated the total number of paired-end relationships between each pair of contigs and analyzed the rates of conflicting and consistent paired ends. A connecting relationship was regarded as a credible relationship between two contigs when that relationship met the given conditions. We then linked the contigs into scaffolds with N bases in a step-by-step manner from the paired-end reads with the shortest insert size to the paired-end reads with the longest insert size. We determined the orientation of the scaffolds on the basis of the paired-end relationship between reads and prescaffolds while we constructed the scaffolds. A reliable link was built between 2 contigs when there were ≥3 paired-end reads for short inserts or ≥5 paired-end reads for long inserts. We started with the smallest libraries, using the RANK parameter of the configuration files to control the order of the libraries. The parameters of this step in the cotton genome assembly were as follows: SOAPdenovo-127mer map –s cotton_0308.lib –p 16 –g GOSakoD –k 39 &gt;map.log; SOAPdenovo-127mer scaff –g GOSakoD –F –p 16 &gt;scaff.log.</p><p><i>Filling gaps</i>. We used GapCloser to fill gaps in the scaffolds. This program makes use of reads for local assembly within the gaps by aligning the other ends of paired-end reads onto scaffolds. The parameters were as follows: GapCloser –a ./GOSakoD.scafSeq –b ./cotton_0330.lib –o ./GOSakoD.scafSeq.FG –t 64.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genetic map construction.</h3><p>Genetic linkage maps were constructed to develop the integrated genome map for anchoring the scaffolds, using 154 individuals from an F<sub>2</sub> population of a cross between <i>G. arboreum</i> cultivars <i>SXY1</i> and <i>AnhuiFuyangZisedahua</i> (<i>AFZ</i>). Young leaves of parents and individuals from the F<sub>2</sub> population were collected for DNA extraction using the Plant Genomics DNA kit (Qiagen). Genomic DNA from this mapping population was digested with the restriction endonuclease EcoRI and processed into RAD libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chutimanitsakun, Y. et al. Construction and application for QTL analysis of a Restriction Site Associated DNA (RAD) linkage map in barley.
