BMC Genomics 12, 4 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2756">38</a></sup>. Every 20 cotton individuals were pooled into 1 sequencing library with nucleotide multiplex identifiers (4, 6 and 8 bp), and each sequencing library was barcoded. Approximately 1,600 Mb of 50-bp reads (10 Mb of read data for each progeny on average) was generated on the HiSeq 2000 next-generation sequencing platform. The SNP calling process was carried out using the SOAP2+SOAPsnp pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053–1059 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2760">39</a></sup>. JoinMap4.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stam, P. Construction of integrated genetic linkage maps by means of a new computer package: JoinMap. Plant J. 3, 739–744 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2763">40</a>) was used to conduct the linkage analysis. A <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> test was used to determine whether the RAD-based SNP markers corresponded with the expected segregation ratio. Unusable markers (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01) were filtered out before a genetic map was constructed. ABH-style markers were used to build the main framework of 13 linkage groups, and a logarithm of odds (LOD) score of 7 was initially set as the linkage threshold for linkage group identification. AC-style markers were also included to expand the length of linkage groups by reducing the LOD value to 3. All high-quality markers were used to construct the consensus maps with the F<sub>2</sub> population in JoinMap4.0. We calculated the recombination fractions between all pairs of SNP markers in a scaffold and chose the SNP marker that had the minimum recombination fraction in the sum. Scaffold order was determined by tag SNPs, and inner SNPs were then used to orient the scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Annotation of transposable elements.</h3><p>We searched the genome for tandem repeats using the software program Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2786">41</a></sup>. Both homology-based and <i>de novo</i> approaches were used to find TEs. The homology-based approach involved applying commonly used databases of known repetitive sequences along with such programs as RepeatProteinMask and RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2793">42</a></sup>. To identify DNA-level TEs, we used Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2797">43</a></sup> along with a database of plant repeating sequences and our <i>de novo</i> TE library to find repeats with RepeatMasker. We used four software programs, LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2805">44</a></sup>, PILER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2809">45</a></sup>, RepeatModeler and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2813">46</a></sup>, to generate the <i>de novo</i> repeat library. These programs predict repeats in different ways. LTR_FINDER retrieves full-length LTR retrotransposons, which usually contain <span class="stix">∼</span>18 bp of sequence that is complementary to the 3′ tail of certain tRNAs. PILER searches for repeats in the genome by aligning the genome with itself. RepeatScout builds a consensus sequence on the basis of lmer using the fit-preferred alignment score. RepeatModeler uses two <i>ab initio</i> repeat prediction programs (RECON and RepeatScout), which identify repeat element boundaries and family relationships among sequences. Intact LTRs were predicted using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2823">47</a></sup> Windows-based software, and solo LTRs were predicted on the basis of comparison with intact ancestor LTRs. Single-copy gene families were used to construct the phylogenetic tree, and syntenic blocks were identified by MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2828">48</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene prediction.</h3><p><i>Homology-based gene prediction</i>. The target locations of homologous proteins were obtained by aligning the protein sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> to the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome using TBLASTN with an <i>E</i>-value parameter of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>. Proteins encoded by TEs were filtered out before alignment. We next extracted target sequences from the genome, including a 2,000-bp extension at both ends of the alignment with intron regions, and again aligned the homologous protein sequences to these DNA fragments using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2872">49</a></sup> (with parameters –trev –sum –genesf). Coding sequences were aligned against the genome using BLAT to generate spliced alignments, and overlapping sequences were filtered out to link the spliced alignments using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2877">50</a></sup>.</p><p><i>De novo gene prediction.</i> AUGUSTUS (version 2.5.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2886">51</a></sup> and SNAP (version 2006-07-28)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2890">52</a></sup> were applied for <i>de novo</i> gene prediction using gene model parameters trained by <i>A. thaliana</i>. We masked all TEs from the genome before gene prediction and filtered out all short coding regions that were &lt;150 bp in length during the process.</p><p><i>Transcriptome alignment.</i> We used TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2905">53</a></sup> to map raw reads of cDNA sequence to the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome sequence to identify potential areas of exons in addition to donor or receptor sites with the following parameters: –p 4-max-intron-length 20,000 –m 1 –r 20-mate-std-dev 20. Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2912">53</a></sup> software was used to assemble potential alignments to transcripts, with the following parameters: –I 20,000 –p 4. To obtain complete gene models, we used the fifth-order Markov model to predict ORFs.</p><p><i>Combination of all methods.</i> Data derived from homology-based, <i>de novo</i> and transcriptome alignment methods were integrated to generate a consensus gene set by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2924">54</a></sup> with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic analysis.</h3><p>Gene Ontology (GO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2936">55</a></sup> terms for each gene were obtained from its corresponding InterPro entry. We used OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2940">56</a></sup> to confirm the genes that were orthologous among four species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>). All-versus-all BLASTP (<i>E</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) comparison of all protein sequences for each species was performed, and orthologous genes were clustered by OrthoMCL (inflation parameter: 1.5). We performed multiple alignments of protein sequences with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2962">57</a></sup> for each single-copy gene family and converted the protein alignments to coding sequences using a Perl script. Phase-1 sites were extracted from each family and concatenated to one supergene for every species, and Mrbayes 3.1.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Ronquist, F. et al. MrBayes 3.2: efficient Bayesian phylogenetic inference and model choice across a large model space. Syst. Biol. 61, 539–542 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2965">58</a>) was used to construct the phylogenetic tree. Branch-specific Ka/Ks ratios (the number of nonsynonymous substitutions per nonsynonymous site, Ka, over the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site, Ks) were calculated using codeml in the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2969">59</a></sup> software package with the branch model.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Syntenic analysis and whole-genome alignment.</h3><p>A BLASTP search (with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) was performed to identify paralogous genes. Syntenic blocks (with at least five genes per block) were identified by MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17936e2986">48</a></sup>. For the alignment results between these, each aligned block represented the orthologous pair derived from the common ancestor, and the sequences that contained the genes were used to show the intergenome relationships with their length information. The fourfold-degenerate value of the blocks was calculated as revised by the HKY model, and whole-genome alignment was carried out by LASTZ (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2987#Sec21">URLs</a>) between <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> after repeat regions were first masked.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">LTR analysis.</h3><p>Intact LTRs from the A genome and D genome were predicted using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3008">47</a></sup> Windows-based software. We constructed ancestor subfamilies on the basis of the following two rules: (i) family members had to share the same polypurine tract sequences and primer-binding-site sequences and (ii) the <i>E</i> value had to be &lt;1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> among transposable gene sequences. For each family, we aligned all intact LTRs with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3017">57</a></sup> and manually corrected the alignments using MEGA5.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731–2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3020">60</a>). We then constructed ancestor sequences for this family using the cons program (contained in the EMBOSS package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3025">61</a></sup>). To find more solo LTRs, we used the ancestor sequences for different families as queries for LTR prediction based on the standard prediction pipeline, and we followed the 80-80-80 rule (identity, &gt;0.8; align rate, &gt;80%; alignment length, &gt;80 bp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3029">62</a></sup> to find which family the solo LTRs belonged to. All intact LTRs and solo LTRs were used to calculate the insert time with the formula time = <i>K</i>/<i>r</i> (where <i>K</i> is the distance between all alignment pairs and <i>r</i> is the rate of nucleotide substitution). The value was set to 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup>, and <i>K</i> was calculated with the distmat program implemented in the EMBOSS package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3051">61</a></sup> with the Kimura two-parameter model.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of disease resistance– and fiber development–related genes.</h3><p>The largest class of characterized disease resistance (R) genes encodes intracellular proteins that contain a nucleotide-binding site (NBS) and C terminus with leucine-rich repeats (LRRs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Meyers, B.C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R.S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129–134 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3063">63</a></sup>. These genes are rather abundant in plant genomes and have an important role in mediating resistance to pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3067">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="DeYoung, B.J. &amp; Innes, R.W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins in pathogen sensing and host defense. Nat. Immunol. 7, 1243–1249 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3070">64</a></sup>. Resistance-related genes were identified using the HMMER 3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Eddy, S.R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3073">65</a>) software package. CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J.P. &amp; Hahn, M.W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2987#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17936e3077">66</a></sup> was used to predict the expansion and contraction of gene numbers on the basis of the topological gene tree.</p><p><i>ACO</i> genes in <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i> were obtained on the basis of a homology search with the <i>ACO</i> genes in <i>G. hirsutum</i>. Dot plot figures were generated using a word size of 9. All fold-change values were calculated with respect to the <i>G. arboreum</i> expression level at 0 dpa. For qRT–PCR analysis, total RNA (<span class="stix">∼</span>2 μg) was reverse transcribed in a 20-μl reaction mixture using SuperScript II reverse transcriptase (Invitrogen). After the reaction, 1-μl aliquots were used as a template for PCR amplification with gene-specific primers. As an internal control, the <i>UBQ7</i> transcript was used to quantify the relative transcript level of each target gene in each tissue type. Three biological replicates were analyzed for all qRT-PCR experiments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">URLs.</h3><p>Cotton Genome Project (CGP), <a href="http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/">http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/</a>; LASTZ, <a href="http://www.bx.psu.edu/miller_lab/">http://www.bx.psu.edu/miller_lab/</a>; RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Accession codes.</h3><p>The <i>G. arboreum</i> genome sequence and gene annotation information have been deposited at the Cotton Genome Project (CGP), available at <a href="http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/">http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/</a>, and all raw sequencing data are accessible through the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA150181">SRA150181</a>.</p></div></div></section>

Wendel, VA. Albert, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="1992" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Wendel, J. &amp; Albert, V.A.                         </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 3 <i>K</i>-mer analysis for estimating the genome size of <i>G. arboreum</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The volume of <i>K</i>-mers is plotted against the frequency at which they occur. The left, truncated peak at low frequency and high volume represents <i>K</i>-mers containing essentially random sequencing errors, whereas the right distribution represents proper (putatively error-free) data. The total <i>K</i>-mer number is 43,099,162,547, and the volume peak is 25. The genome size can be estimated as (total <i>K</i>-mer number)/(volume peak), which is 1,724 Mb.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 4 Linkage groups of the <i>G. arboreum</i> genome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Genetic distances are listed on the left of each group and respective marker IDs are listed on the right.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.2987/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2987/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2987_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 5 Genetic versus physical map distance of the 13 <i>G. arboreum</i> chromosomes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>

                   href="https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.13203"
                   id="title-link-3">
                  A high‐throughput BAC end analysis protocol (
